  Statements of intent to commit high-severity violence; or \n        Calls for high-severity violence including content where no target is specified but a symbol represents the target and\/or against a target. \n     \n      Admissions, statements of intent or advocacy, calls to action, or aspirational or conditional statements to kidnap a target. \n     \n   Threats that lead to serious injury (mid-severity violence) towards private individuals, minor public figures, vulnerable persons, or vulnerable groups where threat is defined as any of the following:\n\n      \n Statements of intent to commit violence; or \n        Statements advocating violence; or \n        Calls for mid-severity violence including content where no target is specified but a symbol represents the target; Aspirational or conditional statements to commit violence \n   \n    \n\n      Threats that lead to physical harm (or other forms of lower-severity violence) towards private individuals (self-reporting required) or minor public figures where threat is defined as any of the following:\n         \n      \n            Private individuals (name and\/or face match are required) or minor        \n      \n    \n     Imagery of private individuals or minor public figures that has been manipulated to include threats of violence either in text or pictorial (adding bulls eye, dart, gun to head, etc.) \n        Any content created for the express purpose of outing an individual as a member of a designated and recognizable at-risk group \n        Instructions on how to make or use weapons if there\u2019s evidence of a goal to seriously injure or kill people, through:\n     \n          \n            Language explicitly stating that goal, or \n            Photos or videos that show or simulate the end result (serious injury or death) as part of the instruction \n            Unless the aforementioned content is shared as part of recreational self defense, for military training purposes, commercial video games, or news coverage (posted by Page or with news logo) \n          \n      \n     \n      Providing instructions on how to make or use explosives:\n         \n          \n            Unless there is clear context that the content is for a non-violent purpose (for example part of commercial video games, clear scientific\/educational purpose, fireworks, or specifically for fishing) \n          \n      \n  \n       Any content containing statements of intent, calls for action, or advocating for high or mid-severity violence due to voting, voter potential offline harm that may be related to content on Facebook. While we understand that people commonly express disdain or disagreement by threatening or calling for violence in non-serious ways, we remove language that incites or facilitates serious violence. We remove content, disable accounts, and work with law enforcement when we believe there is a genuine risk of physical harm or direct threats to public safety. We also try to consider the language and context in order to distinguish casual statements from content that constitutes a credible threat to public or personal safety. In determining whether a threat is credible, we may also consider additional information like a person's public visibility and vulnerability. \n  \n     In some cases, we see aspirational or conditional threats directed at terrorists and other violent actors (e.g. Terrorists deserve to be killed), and we deem those non credible absent specific evidence to the contrary.  \n  \n Page, Group) on our platform: \n    \nTerrorist organizations and terrorists\n   \n \n\t A terrorist organization is defined as:\n  \t \n    \t Any non-governmental organization that engages in \n     \n   \n   A member of a terrorist organization or any person who commits a terrorist act is considered a terrorist\n  \t \n    \t A terrorist act is defined as a premeditated act of violence against persons or property carried out by a non-government actor to intimidate a civilian population, government, or international organization in order to achieve a political, religious, or ideological aim. \n     \n   \n \n \n     \n \nHate organizations and their leaders and prominent members     \n \n   A hate organization is defined as:\n  \t \n    \t Any association of three or more people that is organized under a name, sign, or symbol and that has an ideology, statements, or physical actions that attack individuals based on characteristics, including race, religious affiliation, nationality, ethnicity, gender, sex, sexual orientation, serious disease or disability. \n     \n     \n \n \n     \n Mass and serial murderers\n\t \n     We consider a homicide to be a mass murder if it results in four or more deaths in one incident \n     We consider any individual who has committed two or more murders over multiple incidents or locations a serial murderer \n     We make these assessments based upon the information available to us and will generally apply this policy to a mass or serial murderer who meets any of the following criteria:\n    \t \n     \t    They were convicted of mass or serial murder. \n          They were killed by law enforcement during commission of the mass or serial murder or during subsequent flight. \n          They killed themselves at the scene or in the aftermath of the mass or serial murder. \n          They were identified by law enforcement with images from the crime. \n       \n     \n   \n \n     \n Human slavery, or the removal of organs \n         Recruiting, transporting, transferring, detaining, providing, harboring, or receiving a minor, or an adult against their will \n       \n     \n   \n \n\n Criminal organizations and their leaders and prominent members\n\t \n     A criminal organization is defined as:\n    \t \n      \t Any association of three or more people that is united under a name, color(s), hand gesture(s) or recognized indicia, that has engaged in or threatens to engage in criminal activity, including (but not limited to)\n        \t \n          \t\t Homicide \n               Drug trafficking \n               Arms trafficking \n               Identity theft \n               Money laundering \n               Extortion or trafficking \n               Assault \n               Kidnapping \n             Sexual exploitation (covered in  section 7  and  section 8 ) \n           \n         \n       \n     \n   \n \n     \n      We do not allow symbols that represent any of the above organizations or individuals to be shared on our platform without context that condemns or neutrally discusses the content. \n     \n      We do not allow content that praises any of the above organizations or individuals or any acts committed by them. \n     \n      We do not allow coordination of support for any of the above organizations or individuals or any acts committed by them. \n    \n  \n ","policy_rationale":"\n   \nIn an effort to prevent and disrupt real-world harm, we do not allow any organizations or individuals that proclaim a violent mission or are engaged in violence, from having a presence on Facebook. This includes organizations or individuals involved in the following:\n \n \n   Terrorist activity \n   Organized hate \n   Mass or serial murder \n   Human trafficking \n   Organized violence or criminal activity \n   \n   \n     We also remove content that expresses support or praise for groups, leaders, or individuals involved in these activities. We also prohibit the purchase, sale, gifting, exchange, and transfer of firearms, including firearm parts or ammunition, between private individuals on Facebook. Some of these items are not regulated everywhere; however, because of the borderless nature of our community, we try to enforce our policies as consistently as possible. Firearm stores and online retailers may promote items available for sale off of our services as long as those retailers comply with all applicable laws and regulations. We allow discussions about sales of firearms and firearm parts in stores or by online retailers and advocating for changes to firearm regulation. Self-injury is defined as the intentional and direct injuring of the body, including self-mutilation and eating disorders. We want Facebook to be a space where people can share their experiences, raise awareness about these issues, and seek support from one another, which is why we allow people to discuss suicide and self-injury.\n    \n We work with organizations around the world to provide assistance to people in distress. We also talk to experts in suicide and self-injury to help inform our policies and enforcement. For example, we have been advised by experts that we should not remove live videos of self-injury while there is an opportunity for loved ones and authorities to provide help or resources. \n\n In contrast, we remove any content that identifies and negatively targets victims or survivors of self-injury or suicide seriously, humorously, or rhetorically. \n\n     Learn more about our suicide and personally identifiable information, to sending threatening messages, and making unwanted malicious contact. We do not tolerate this kind of behavior because it prevents people from feeling safe and respected on Facebook. \n\n We distinguish between public figures and private individuals because we want to allow discussion, which often includes critical commentary of people who are featured in the news or who have a large public audience. For public figures, we remove attacks that are severe as well as certain attacks where the public figure is directly tagged in the post or comment. For private individuals, our protection goes further: we remove content that's meant to degrade or shame, including, for example, claims about someone's sexual activity. We recognize that bullying and harassment can have more of an emotional impact on minors, which is why our policies provide heightened protection for users between the ages of 13 and 18. \n\n Context and intent matter, and we allow people to share and re-share posts if it is clear that something was shared in order to condemn or draw attention to bullying and harassment. In certain instances, we require self-reporting because it helps us understand that the person targeted feels bullied or harassed. In addition to reporting such behavior and content, we encourage people to use  tools available on Facebook  to help protect against it. \n    \n     We also have a  Bullying Prevention Hub , which is a resource for teens, parents, and educators seeking support for issues related to bullying and other conflicts. It offers step-by-step guidance, including information on how to start important conversations about bullying. Facebook because it creates an environment of intimidation and exclusion and in some cases may promote real-world violence. \n\n     We define hate speech as a direct attack on people based on what we call protected characteristics \u2014 race, ethnicity, national origin, religious affiliation, sexual orientation, caste, sex, gender, gender identity, and serious disease or disability. We also provide some protections for immigration status. We define attack as violent or dehumanizing speech, statements of inferiority, or calls for exclusion or segregation. We separate attacks into three tiers of severity, as described below. \n\n     Sometimes people share content containing someone else\u2019s hate speech for the purpose of raising awareness or educating others. In some cases, words or terms that might otherwise violate our standards are used self-referentially or in an empowering way. People sometimes express contempt in the context of a romantic break-up. Other times, they use gender-exclusive language to control membership in a health or positive support group, such as a breastfeeding group for women only. In all of these cases, we allow the content but expect people to clearly indicate their intent, which helps us better understand why they shared it. Where the intention is unclear, we may remove the content. \n\n     We allow humor and social commentary related to these topics. In addition, we believe that people are more responsible when they share this kind of commentary using their authentic identity. \n\n     Click here to read our Hard Questions Blog  and learn more about our approach to hate speech. \n \n "},"graphic_violence":{"other_content":"\n   \n  \n    Do not post: \n    \n     Imagery of violence committed against real people or animals with comments or captions by the poster or comments under such imagery that contain\n      \n      \n        Enjoyment of suffering \n        Enjoyment of humiliation \n        Erotic response to suffering \n        Remarks that speak positively of the violence; or \n        Remarks indicating the poster is sharing footage for sensational viewing pleasure \n      \n\t\t \n     \n Videos of dying, wounded, or dead people if they contain\n   \n  \t Dismemberment unless in a medical setting \n     Visible internal organs; partially decomposed bodies \n     Charred or burning people unless in the context of cremation or self-immolation when that action is a form of political speech or newsworthy \n     Victims of cannibalism \n     Throat-slitting \n        \n \n      \n       Videos or photos that depict non-sexual child abuse, defined as\n   \n     Repeated kicking, beating, slapping, or stepping on by an adult or animal \n     Strangling or suffocating by an adult or animal \n     Drowning by an adult or animal \n     Biting through skin by an adult or animal \n     Poisoning by an adult \n     Forcible restraint by an adult \n     Inflicting of burn or cut wounds by an adult \n     Forcible smoking \n Tossing, rotating, or shaking of an infant (too young to stand) by their wrists\/ankles, arms\/legs, or neck \n   \n      \n     Videos and photos showing violent death of persons can be removed if requested by a family member.    \n \n     \n    \n  \n   \n      \n  For the following content, we include a warning screen so that people are aware the content may be disturbing. We want our policies to be based on feedback from community representatives and a broad spectrum of the people who use our service, and we want to learn from and incorporate the advice of experts.  \n          Engagement makes our Community Standards stronger and more inclusive. It brings our stakeholders more fully into the policy development process, introduces us to new perspectives, allows us to share our thinking on policy options, and roots our policies in sources of knowledge and experience that go beyond Facebook.  \n          Product Policy is the team that writes the rules for what people are allowed to share on Facebook, including the Community Standards. To open up the policy development process and gather outside views on our policies, we created the Stakeholder Engagement team, a sub-team that\u2019s part of Product Policy. Stakeholder Engagement's main goal is to ensure that our policy development process is informed by the views of outside experts and the people who use Facebook. We have developed specific practices and a structure for engagement in the context of the Community Standards, and we\u2019re expanding our work to cover additional policies, particularly ads policies and major News Feed ranking changes.  \n       In this post, we provide an overview of how stakeholder engagement contributes to the Community Standards. While Facebook is of course responsible for the substance of its policies, engagement helps us improve those policies and deepen important stakeholder relationships in ways we\u2019ll explain. To benchmark our work and help us incorporate best practices in this area, we retained non-profit organization BSR (Business for Social Responsibility). BSR conducted an analysis that has informed our perspective on engagement, and that we\u2019ve used in preparing this post. \n       \n      \n      \n       WHO ARE OUR STAKEHOLDERS? \n       By \u201cstakeholders\u201d we mean all organizations and individuals who are impacted by, and therefore have a stake in, Facebook\u2019s Community Standards. Because the Community Standards apply to every post, photo, and video shared on Facebook, this means that our more than 2.7 billion users are, in a broad sense, stakeholders.  \n       But we can\u2019t meaningfully engage with that many people. So it\u2019s also useful to think of stakeholders as those who are informed about and able to speak on behalf of others. This is why the primary focus of our engagement is civil society organizations, activist groups, and thought leaders, in such areas as digital and civil rights, anti-discrimination, free speech, and human rights. We also engage with academics who have relevant expertise. Academics may not directly represent the interests of others, but they are important stakeholders by virtue of their extensive knowledge, which helps us create better policies for everyone. \n      \n      \n       HOW DO WE INCORPORATE STAKEHOLDER ENGAGEMENT INTO OUR POLICY DEVELOPMENT PROCESS? \n       Integrating stakeholder feedback into the policy-making process is a core part of how we work. Though it's important that we not over-promise, we know that what stakeholders seek above all is for their insights to inform our policy decisions.  \n        There are many reasons why we may draft a new policy or revise an existing one. We continuously build our policies to meet the needs of our community. Sometimes external stakeholders tell us that a particular policy fails to address an issue that\u2019s important to them. In other cases, the press draws attention to a policy gap. Often, members of Facebook\u2019s Community Operations team (whose employees, contractors, and out-sourcing partners are responsible for enforcing the Community Standards) tell us about trends or the need for policy clarification. And Facebook\u2019s Research teams (both within Product Policy and in other parts of the company) may point us to data or user sentiment that seems best addressed through policy-making. \n        In considering a new policy, Product Policy runs a series of internal working groups, leading up to a cross-team meeting we call the Product Policy Forum (previously referred to as the Content Standards Forum). We\u2019ve explained the Content Standards Forum in detail  here . \n        At the outset, the Stakeholder Engagement team frames up policy questions requiring feedback and determines what types of stakeholders to prioritize for engagement. We then reach out to external stakeholders, gathering feedback that we document and synthesize for our colleagues.  \n        Our engagement on the Community Standards takes many forms. The heart of our approach to engagement is private conversations, most often in person or by video-conference. We\u2019ve found that this approach lends itself to candid dialogue and relationship-building. We typically don't release the names of those we engage with because conversations can be sensitive and we want to ensure open lines of communication. Some stakeholders may also request or require confidentiality, particularly if media attention is unwanted or if they are members of a vulnerable community. \n        In addition, we sometimes convene group discussions, bringing together stakeholders in particular regions or specific policy areas. We\u2019ve found the group setting to be useful for generating ideas and providing updates to multiple stakeholders. And on occasion it also makes sense to reach out to relevant Facebook users to get their views. Recently, for example, we reviewed the \u201cexclusion\u201d element of our hate speech policy. As part of this process, we talked to the admins from a number of major Facebook Groups (admins are responsible for managing Group settings), who shared their insights with us relating to this policy. We'll do more of this user outreach in the future.  \n        In our conversations with external stakeholders, we share Facebook's thinking on the proposed policy change, including what led us to reconsider this policy, and the pros and cons of policy options we've identified. The feedback we receive is fed into the process and shapes our ongoing deliberations by highlighting new perspectives and helping us evaluate our options. When stakeholder views conflict, we analyze the spectrum of opinion and points of disagreement. We want to identify which views are most persuasive and instructive for us, but we\u2019re not necessarily trying to reconcile them; rather, our goal is to understand the full range of opinion concerning the proposal. In some cases we return to stakeholders for additional input as our thinking develops.  \n       At the Product Policy Forum, the Stakeholder Engagement team presents a detailed summary of the feedback we've received on each policy proposal, and we lay out the views of our stakeholders on a spectrum of policy options. This summary is made public (minus the names of individual stakeholders and organizations) in our published  minutes  of the meeting. In this way, anyone can see the range and nature of engagement we\u2019ve conducted for policy proposals, and the rationale for our final decision. After policy development is complete, we inform our stakeholders what we\u2019ve decided. \n      \n      \n      \n       WHAT PRINCIPLES GUIDE OUR ENGAGEMENT? \n       A commitment to stakeholder engagement means addressing a number of essential questions \u2014 such as, how do we decide which groups and individuals to talk to, how do we make sure that vulnerable groups are heard, and how do we find relevant experts? \n       Our policies involve a complex balancing of values such as safety, voice, and equity. There\u2019s no simple formula for how engagement contributes to this work. But over the past year we\u2019ve developed a structure and methodology for engagement on our Community Standards, built around three core principles: \n      \n       Inclusiveness \n       Engagement broadens our perspective and creates a more inclusive approach to policy-making. \n      \n        Engagement helps us better understand how our policies impact those who use our service. When we make decisions about what content to remove and what to leave up, we affect people's speech and the way they connect on Facebook. Not everyone will agree on where we draw the lines, but at a minimum, we need to understand the concerns of those who are affected by our policies. This is particularly important for stakeholders whose voices have been marginalized. \n       Understanding how our policies affect the people who use Facebook presents a major challenge. Our scale makes it impossible to speak directly to the more than 2.7 billion people who use our platform. This dilemma also underscores the importance of reaching out to a broad spectrum of stakeholders in all regions so that our policy-making process is globally diverse. \n        The question of our impact plays out in many ways. Our policies are global (the Internet is borderless, and our mission is to build community), but we touch people's lives on a very local level. We are often asked, \u201cWhy should  you  be creating policies to govern online speech for  me ?\u201d Typically embedded in this question is a demand that we show more cultural sensitivity and understanding of regional context.  \n        Stakeholder engagement gives us a tool to deepen our local knowledge and perspective \u2013 so we can hear voices we might otherwise miss. For each policy proposal, we identify a global and diverse set of stakeholders on the issue. We seek voices across the policy spectrum, but it\u2019s not always self-evident what the spectrum is. In many cases, our policies don't line up neatly with traditional dichotomies, such as liberal versus conservative, or civil libertarian versus law enforcement. We talk to others in Facebook's Policy and Research organizations and conduct our own research to identify a range of diverse stakeholders. \n        For example, in considering how our hate speech policy should apply to certain forms of gendered language, we spoke with academic experts, women's and digital rights groups, and free speech advocates. Likewise, when considering our policy on nudity and sexual activity in art, we listened to family safety organizations, artists, and museum curators. In reviewing how our policies should apply to memorialized profiles of deceased users, we connected both with professors who study digital legacy as an academic subject and Facebook users who've been designated as \u201cLegacy Contacts\u201d and who have real world experience with this product feature. \n       It's not enough to ask how our policies affect users in general. We need to understand how our policies will impact people who are particularly vulnerable by virtue of laws, cultural practices, poverty, or other reasons that prevent them from speaking up for their rights. In our stakeholder mapping, we seek to put an emphasis on minority groups that have traditionally lacked power, such as political dissidents and religious minorities throughout the world. In reevaluating how our hate speech policy applies to certain behavioral generalizations, for example, we consulted with immigrants rights groups. Our efforts are a work in progress, but we are committed to bringing these voices into our policy discussions. \n      \n       Expertise       \n       Engagement brings expertise to our policy development process. \n        The Stakeholder Engagement team conducts detailed, iterative research to identify top subject matter experts in civil society and academia. It then gathers their views to inform our policy decisions.  \n       This engagement ensures that our policy-making process is informed by current theories and analysis, empirical research, and an understanding of the latest online trends. The expertise we gather includes issues of language, social identity, and geography, all of which bear on our policies in important ways.  \n       Facebook\u2019s policies are entwined with many complex social and technological issues, such as hate speech, terrorism, bullying and harassment, and threats of violence. Sometimes we're looking for guidance on how safety and voice should be balanced \u2014 for example, in considering what types of speech to allow about \u201cpublic figures\u201d under our policies. In other cases, we're reaching out to gain specialized knowledge, such as how our policies can draw on international human rights principles, or how minority communities may experience certain types of speech. \n        We don\u2019t have all the answers to address these problems on our platform. Sometimes the challenges we face are novel even to the experts we consult with. But by talking to outside experts and incorporating their feedback, we make our policies more thoughtful. \n       For example, over the past year and a half we've modified our  hate speech policy  to recognize three tiers of attacks. Tier 1, the most severe, involves calls to violence or dehumanizing speech against other people based on their race, ethnicity, nationality, gender or other protected characteristic (\u201cKill the Christians\u201d). Tier 2 attacks consist of statements of inferiority or expressions of contempt or disgust (\u201cMexicans are lazy\u201d). And Tier 3 covers calls to exclude or segregate (\u201cNo women allowed\u201d).  \n        Developing these tiers has made our policies more nuanced and precise. On the basis of the tiers, we're able to provide additional protections against the most harmful forms of speech. For instance, we now remove Tier 1 hate speech directed against immigrants (\u201cimmigrants are rats\u201d) but permit less intense forms of speech (\u201cimmigrants should stay out of our country\u201d) to leave room for broad political discourse.  \n        As part of our work we spoke to outside experts \u2014 academics, NGOs that study hate speech, and groups all across the political landscape. This engagement helped confirm that the tiers were comprehensive and aligned with patterns of online and offline behavior. We\u2019ll continue to consider adjustments to our policies in light of opinions from experts and civil society. \n      \n       Transparency       \n       Engagement makes our policies and our policy development process more transparent. \n       Given the impact of our Community Standards on society, it\u2019s critical for us to create a policy development process that\u2019s not only inclusive and based on expert knowledge, but also transparent. We know from talking to hundreds of stakeholders that opening up our policy-making process helps build trust. The more visibility we provide, the more our stakeholders are likely to view the Community Standards as relevant, legitimate, and based on consent.  \n       When we engage, we share details about the challenges of moderating content for 2.7 billion people and we explain the rationale behind our policies and why there may be a need for improvement. We gather stakeholder feedback so we can develop creative policy solutions to these problems. The policies we launch based on this process are still owned by Facebook, but they are stronger by virtue of having been tested through consultation and an exchange of views.  \n        It\u2019s important to acknowledge that our policies will never make everyone happy. Nudity, say, is viewed quite differently in Scandinavia and Southeast Asia, and no Facebook policy on nudity would be equally satisfactory to both. Our job as a team is to craft thoughtful global policies, knowing that our work will be criticized by some.   \n       Transparency on our process of engagement also helps us build a system of rules and enforcement that people regard as fair. We know that some people would like us to go further and disclose the names of our stakeholders and even the substance of our discussions with them. For reasons discussed above, we\u2019ve chosen not to go this route, at least for now. We'll continue to experiment with ways of being more public about engagement where we have the prior agreement of our stakeholders. \n      \n      \n     THE WAY FORWARD \n       As the breadth and specialization of our policies increase, so too will the scope of our engagement. We\u2019ll continue to refine our policy development process and work to realize our stakeholder engagement principles of inclusiveness, expertise, and transparency. We expect our team to grow, and our reach to expand.  \n       With BSR\u2019s help, we\u2019re also working on specific ways to improve.  \n       For example, we'll continue to develop means to engage with new stakeholders around the world and will seek guidance from regional experts about how to do so most effectively. We\u2019ve also had requests to explore channels like informal roundtables and recurring video-conference meetings as a way of staying in touch with stakeholders on specific policy issues. These settings provide continuity and enable us to involve stakeholders even more closely in the design of specific policies and products. \n       As we expand the scope of our outreach, we also want to investigate whether we might be able to create other mechanisms for users to give us feedback on our policies. For example, as part of the effort to gather global feedback on the Oversight Board, Facebook created a  public consultation process  containing both a questionnaire and free-form questions. Through this tool, users were able to submit their views on the Board directly to Facebook. One could imagine a similar process whereby NGOs and civil society organizations could join our network of contacts in order to receive regular policy updates and provide feedback to members of our team.   \n       These are all exciting challenges, and we look forward to working with our stakeholders to improve the level of our engagement and its contribution to the development of our policies. \n      \n     \n   \n ","policy_rationale":null}},"rawIntroContent":"\n   \n    Every day, people come to Facebook to share their stories, see the world through the eyes of others, and connect with friends and causes. The conversations that happen on Facebook reflect the diversity of a community of more than two billion people communicating across countries and cultures and in dozens of languages, posting everything from text to photos and videos. \n   We recognize how important it is for Facebook to be a place where people feel empowered to communicate, and we take our role in keeping abuse off our service seriously. That\u2019s why we have developed a set of Community Standards that outline what is and is not allowed on Facebook. Our Standards apply around the world to all types of content. They\u2019re designed to be comprehensive \u2013 for example, content that might not be considered hate speech may still be removed for violating our bullying policies. \n\n     The goal of our Community Standards is to  encourage expression and create a safe environment . We  develop our policies  based on input from our community and from experts in fields such as technology and public safety. Our policies are also rooted in the following principles: \n   \n      \n     Safety:  People need to feel safe in order to build community. We are committed to removing content that encourages real-world harm, including (but not limited to) physical, financial, and emotional injury. \n  \n        \n  \n Voice:  Our mission is all about embracing diverse views. We err on the side of allowing content, even when some find it objectionable, unless removing that content can prevent a specific harm. Moreover, at times we will allow content that might otherwise violate our standards if we feel that it is newsworthy, significant, or important to the public interest. We do this only after weighing the public interest value of the content against the risk of real-world harm. \n  \n    \n      \n Equity:  Our community is global and diverse. Our policies may seem broad, but that is because we apply them consistently and fairly to a community that transcends regions, cultures, and languages. As a result, our Community Standards can sometimes appear less nuanced than we would like, leading to an outcome that is at odds with their underlying purpose. For that reason, in some cases, and when we are provided with additional context, we make a decision based on the spirit, rather than the letter, of the policy.\n \n\n    \n     Everyone on Facebook plays a part in keeping the platform safe and respectful. We ask people to share responsibly and to let us know when they see something that may violate our Community Standards. We make it easy for people to report potentially violating content, including Pages, Groups, profiles, individual content, and\/or comments to us for review. We also give people the option to  block ,  unfollow , or  hide  people and posts, so that they can control their own experience on Facebook. \n\n     The consequences for violating our Community Standards vary depending on the severity of the violation and a person's history on the platform. For instance, we may warn someone for a first violation, but if they continue to violate our policies, we may restrict their ability to post on Facebook or disable their profile. We also may notify law enforcement when we believe there is a genuine risk of physical harm or a direct threat to public safety.  \n\n     Our Community Standards, which we will continue to develop over time, serve as a guide for how to communicate on Facebook. It is in this spirit that we ask members of the Facebook community to follow these guidelines. \n    \n potentiellement li\u00e9 \u00e0 du contenu sur Facebook. Bien qu\u2019il soit commun pour des utilisateurs d\u2019exprimer leur d\u00e9dain ou leur d\u00e9saccord sous forme de menaces ou d\u2019appels \u00e0 la violence, mais de mani\u00e8re non s\u00e9rieuse, nous supprimons les propos qui encouragent ou permettent des violences graves. Lorsque nous pensons qu\u2019un contenu repr\u00e9sente un r\u00e9el risque de pr\u00e9judice physique ou une atteinte directe \u00e0 la s\u00e9curit\u00e9 publique, nous supprimons le contenu, d\u00e9sactivons les comptes concern\u00e9s et collaborons avec les autorit\u00e9s. Nous essayons \u00e9galement d\u2019examiner le langage utilis\u00e9 et le contexte pour distinguer les d\u00e9clarations triviales de celles qui repr\u00e9sentent une menace r\u00e9elle pour la s\u00e9curit\u00e9 publique ou celle d\u2019un individu. Pour d\u00e9terminer la cr\u00e9dibilit\u00e9 d\u2019une menace, nous examinons \u00e9ventuellement d\u2019autres informations, telles que le degr\u00e9 d\u2019exposition publique et de terroristes et les terroristes\n   \n \n\t Une organisation terroriste correspond \u00e0\u00a0:\n  \t \n    \t Toute organisation non gouvernementale impliqu\u00e9e dans des actes de violence pr\u00e9m\u00e9dit\u00e9s contre des individus ou une propri\u00e9t\u00e9 en vue d\u2019intimider une population civile, un gouvernement ou un organisme international et avec pour objectif comme des terroristes\n  \t \n    \t Un acte terroriste correspond \u00e0 tout acte de violence pr\u00e9m\u00e9dit\u00e9 contre des individus ou une propri\u00e9t\u00e9, perp\u00e9tr\u00e9 par une personne n\u2019appartenant pas \u00e0 un gouvernement en vue d\u2019intimider une population civile, un gouvernement ou un organisme international, avec pour objectif d\u2019atteindre un but anim\u00e9e par la haine correspond \u00e0\u00a0:\n  \t \n    \t Toute association de trois personnes ou plus organis\u00e9es sous un nom ou un symbole et dont l\u2019id\u00e9ologie, les d\u00e9clarations ou les actions physiques portent atteinte \u00e0 des individus en fonction de caract\u00e9ristiques, notamment la race, l\u2019affiliation religieuse, la nationalit\u00e9, l\u2019ethnicit\u00e9, le sexe, l\u2019orientation sexuelle, une maladie grave ou un handicap. \n     \n     \n \n \n     \n Les meurtriers de masse et en s\u00e9rie\n\t \n     Nous consid\u00e9rons qu\u2019un homicide est un meurtre de masse si au moins quatre d\u00e9c\u00e8s sont \u00e0 d\u00e9plorer dans le cadre d\u2019un seul incident \n     Nous consid\u00e9rons tout individu ayant commis au moins deux meurtres au cours de plusieurs incidents ou \u00e0 plusieurs endroits comme un meurtrier en s\u00e9rie \n     Nous proc\u00e9dons \u00e0 ces \u00e9valuations en fonction des informations \u00e0 notre disposition et appliquons g\u00e9n\u00e9ralement cette r\u00e8gle \u00e0 tout meurtrier de masse ou en s\u00e9rie qui correspond aux crit\u00e8res suivants\u00a0:\n    \t \n     \t    L\u2019individu a \u00e9t\u00e9 condamn\u00e9 pour meurtre de masse ou en s\u00e9rie. \n          L\u2019individu a \u00e9t\u00e9 ex\u00e9cut\u00e9 par les forces de l\u2019ordre pendant la perp\u00e9tration du meurtre de masse ou en s\u00e9rie, ou au cours d\u2019une fuite en r\u00e9sultant. \n          L\u2019individu s\u2019est donn\u00e9 la mort sur place \u00e0 la suite du meurtre de masse ou en s\u00e9rie. \n          L\u2019individu a \u00e9t\u00e9 identifi\u00e9 par les forces de l\u2019ordre gr\u00e2ce \u00e0 des photos du crime. \n       \n     \n   \n \n     \n Les groupes impliqu\u00e9s dans la traite des personnes et leurs dirigeants\n\t \n     Les groupes impliqu\u00e9s dans la traite des personnes sont des organismes responsables des actes suivants\u00a0:\n    \t \n      \t Prostitution d\u2019autrui, travail forc\u00e9\/asservi, esclavage ou pr\u00e9l\u00e8vement d\u2019organes \n         Recrutement, transport, transfert, d\u00e9tention, mise \u00e0 disposition, h\u00e9bergement ou r\u00e9ception d\u2019un mineur ou d\u2019un adulte contre sa volont\u00e9 \n       \n     \n   \n \n\n Les organisations criminelles et leurs dirigeants et membres pro\u00e9minents\n\t \n     Une organisation criminelle correspond \u00e0\u00a0:\n    \t \n      \t Toute association de trois personnes ou plus, unie sous un nom, une ou plusieurs couleurs, un ou plusieurs gestes de la main ou une marque de reconnaissance, qui participe ou menace de participer \u00e0 des activit\u00e9s criminelles, et notamment (sans s\u2019y limiter) aux activit\u00e9s suivantes\u00a0:\n        \t \n          \t\t Homicide \n               Trafic de drogue \n               Trafic d\u2019armes \n               Usurpation d\u2019identit\u00e9 \n               Blanchiment d\u2019argent \n               Extorsion ou trafic \n               Agression \n               Kidnapping \n             Exploitation sexuelle (abord\u00e9e aux  sections\u00a07  et  8 ) \n           \n         \n       \n     \n   \n \n     \n      Nous n\u2019autorisons pas le partage de symboles repr\u00e9sentant l\u2019une des organisations ou l\u2019un des individus ci-dessus sur notre plate-forme sans un contexte condamnant le contenu ou en discutant de fa\u00e7on neutre. \n     \n      Nous interdisons tout contenu pr\u00f4nant l\u2019une des organisations ou l\u2019un des individus ci-dessus ou tout acte commis par eux. \n     \n      Nous interdisons la coordination de soutien en faveur des organisations ou individus ci-dessus ou de tout acte commis par eux. \n    \n  \n ","policy_rationale":"\n   \nAfin d\u2019\u00e9viter et d\u2019emp\u00eacher toute nuisance et tout danger r\u00e9el, les organisations ou individus qui revendiquent des objectifs violents ou qui sont impliqu\u00e9s dans des activit\u00e9s violentes ne sont pas les bienvenus sur Facebook\u00a0: Cela comprend les organisations et les individus impliqu\u00e9s dans les activit\u00e9s suivantes\u00a0:\n \n \n   Les activit\u00e9s terroristes \n   La haine organis\u00e9e \n   Les meurtres de masse ou en s\u00e9rie \n   La traite des personnes \n   La violence ou les activit\u00e9s criminelles organis\u00e9es \n   \n   \n     Nous supprimons \u00e9galement tout contenu soutenant ou faisant l\u2019\u00e9loge de groupes, dirigeants ou individus impliqu\u00e9s dans ces activit\u00e9s. ","policy_rationale":"\n   Le harc\u00e8lement et l\u2019intimidation se produisent en divers lieux et sous diff\u00e9rentes formes, qu\u2019il s\u2019agisse d\u2019une menace de diffusion d\u2019informations personnelles identifiables, de l\u2019envoi de messages de menace ou de contacts malveillants ind\u00e9sir\u00e9s. Nous ne tol\u00e9rons pas ce type de comportement, car il emp\u00eache les gens de se sentir respect\u00e9s et en s\u00e9curit\u00e9 sur Facebook. \n\n Nous distinguons les personnalit\u00e9s publiques des personnes priv\u00e9es, car notre plate-forme pr\u00f4ne l\u2019\u00e9change, ce qui implique souvent des commentaires critiques concernant des individus qui attirent l\u2019attention des m\u00e9dias ou du grand public. Concernant les personnalit\u00e9s publiques, nous supprimons les attaques graves et certaines attaques qui identifient directement la personnalit\u00e9 publique dans une publication ou un commentaire. Concernant les personnes priv\u00e9es, notre protection est plus large\u00a0: nous supprimons le contenu visant \u00e0 rabaisser une personne ou \u00e0 la couvrir de honte, et, notamment, les d\u00e9clarations relatives aux activit\u00e9s sexuelles de quelqu\u2019un. Nous reconnaissons que l\u2019intimidation et le harc\u00e8lement peuvent avoir des r\u00e9percussions \u00e9motionnelles plus graves pour les mineurs. C\u2019est pourquoi nos politiques offrent une protection plus \u00e9lev\u00e9e pour les utilisateurs \u00e2g\u00e9s de 13\u00a0\u00e0 18\u00a0ans. \n\n Le contexte et l\u2019intention importent\u00a0; nous autorisons les personnes \u00e0 partager et \u00e0 repartager des publications si le contenu partag\u00e9 vise clairement \u00e0 condamner l\u2019intimidation et le harc\u00e8lement ou \u00e0 attirer l\u2019attention sur ces comportements. Dans certains cas, nous demandons que la personne cibl\u00e9e fasse elle-m\u00eame le signalement, car cela nous permet de savoir qu\u2019elle se sent intimid\u00e9e et harcel\u00e9e. En plus de signaler de tels comportements et contenus, nous encourageons les gens \u00e0 utiliser  les outils disponibles sur Facebook  pour se prot\u00e9ger contre ceux-ci. \n    \n     Nous avons \u00e9galement une  plate-forme de pr\u00e9vention contre l\u2019intimidation , qui est une ressource destin\u00e9e aux adolescents, aux parents et aux \u00e9ducateurs cherchant un soutien pour les questions li\u00e9es \u00e0 l\u2019intimidation et \u00e0 d\u2019autres conflits. Elle offre des conseils pas \u00e0 pas, y compris des informations sur la mani\u00e8re d\u2019ouvrir des discussions importantes concernant l\u2019intimidation. Apprenez-en davantage sur les mesures que nous prenons pour prot\u00e9ger les gens contre le harc\u00e8lement et l\u2019intimidation  ici . \n   \n Nous d\u00e9finissons une attaque comme un discours violent ou d\u00e9shumanisant, une affirmation d\u2019inf\u00e9riorit\u00e9, ou un appel \u00e0 l\u2019exclusion ou \u00e0 la s\u00e9gr\u00e9gation. We want our policies to be based on feedback from community representatives and a broad spectrum of the people who use our service, and we want to learn from and incorporate the advice of experts.  \n          Engagement makes our Community Standards stronger and more inclusive. It brings our stakeholders more fully into the policy development process, introduces us to new perspectives, allows us to share our thinking on policy options, and roots our policies in sources of knowledge and experience that go beyond Facebook.  \n          Product Policy is the team that writes the rules for what people are allowed to share on Facebook, including the Community Standards. To open up the policy development process and gather outside views on our policies, we created the Stakeholder Engagement team, a sub-team that\u2019s part of Product Policy. Stakeholder Engagement's main goal is to ensure that our policy development process is informed by the views of outside experts and the people who use Facebook. We have developed specific practices and a structure for engagement in the context of the Community Standards, and we\u2019re expanding our work to cover additional policies, particularly ads policies and major News Feed ranking changes.  \n       In this post, we provide an overview of how stakeholder engagement contributes to the Community Standards. While Facebook is of course responsible for the substance of its policies, engagement helps us improve those policies and deepen important stakeholder relationships in ways we\u2019ll explain. To benchmark our work and help us incorporate best practices in this area, we retained non-profit organization BSR (Business for Social Responsibility). BSR conducted an analysis that has informed our perspective on engagement, and that we\u2019ve used in preparing this post. \n       \n      \n      \n       WHO ARE OUR STAKEHOLDERS? \n       By \u201cstakeholders\u201d we mean all organizations and individuals who are impacted by, and therefore have a stake in, Facebook\u2019s Community Standards. Because the Community Standards apply to every post, photo, and video shared on Facebook, this means that our more than 2.7 billion users are, in a broad sense, stakeholders.  \n       But we can\u2019t meaningfully engage with that many people. So it\u2019s also useful to think of stakeholders as those who are informed about and able to speak on behalf of others. This is why the primary focus of our engagement is civil society organizations, activist groups, and thought leaders, in such areas as digital and civil rights, anti-discrimination, free speech, and human rights. We also engage with academics who have relevant expertise. Academics may not directly represent the interests of others, but they are important stakeholders by virtue of their extensive knowledge, which helps us create better policies for everyone. \n      \n      \n       HOW DO WE INCORPORATE STAKEHOLDER ENGAGEMENT INTO OUR POLICY DEVELOPMENT PROCESS? \n       Integrating stakeholder feedback into the policy-making process is a core part of how we work. Though it's important that we not over-promise, we know that what stakeholders seek above all is for their insights to inform our policy decisions.  \n        There are many reasons why we may draft a new policy or revise an existing one. We continuously build our policies to meet the needs of our community. Sometimes external stakeholders tell us that a particular policy fails to address an issue that\u2019s important to them. In other cases, the press draws attention to a policy gap. Often, members of Facebook\u2019s Community Operations team (whose employees, contractors, and out-sourcing partners are responsible for enforcing the Community Standards) tell us about trends or the need for policy clarification. And Facebook\u2019s Research teams (both within Product Policy and in other parts of the company) may point us to data or user sentiment that seems best addressed through policy-making. \n        In considering a new policy, Product Policy runs a series of internal working groups, leading up to a cross-team meeting we call the Product Policy Forum (previously referred to as the Content Standards Forum). We\u2019ve explained the Content Standards Forum in detail  here . \n        At the outset, the Stakeholder Engagement team frames up policy questions requiring feedback and determines what types of stakeholders to prioritize for engagement. We then reach out to external stakeholders, gathering feedback that we document and synthesize for our colleagues.  \n        Our engagement on the Community Standards takes many forms. The heart of our approach to engagement is private conversations, most often in person or by video-conference. We\u2019ve found that this approach lends itself to candid dialogue and relationship-building. We typically don't release the names of those we engage with because conversations can be sensitive and we want to ensure open lines of communication. Some stakeholders may also request or require confidentiality, particularly if media attention is unwanted or if they are members of a vulnerable community. \n        In addition, we sometimes convene group discussions, bringing together stakeholders in particular regions or specific policy areas. We\u2019ve found the group setting to be useful for generating ideas and providing updates to multiple stakeholders. And on occasion it also makes sense to reach out to relevant Facebook users to get their views. Recently, for example, we reviewed the \u201cexclusion\u201d element of our hate speech policy. As part of this process, we talked to the admins from a number of major Facebook Groups (admins are responsible for managing Group settings), who shared their insights with us relating to this policy. We'll do more of this user outreach in the future.  \n        In our conversations with external stakeholders, we share Facebook's thinking on the proposed policy change, including what led us to reconsider this policy, and the pros and cons of policy options we've identified. The feedback we receive is fed into the process and shapes our ongoing deliberations by highlighting new perspectives and helping us evaluate our options. When stakeholder views conflict, we analyze the spectrum of opinion and points of disagreement. We want to identify which views are most persuasive and instructive for us, but we\u2019re not necessarily trying to reconcile them; rather, our goal is to understand the full range of opinion concerning the proposal. In some cases we return to stakeholders for additional input as our thinking develops.  \n       At the Product Policy Forum, the Stakeholder Engagement team presents a detailed summary of the feedback we've received on each policy proposal, and we lay out the views of our stakeholders on a spectrum of policy options. This summary is made public (minus the names of individual stakeholders and organizations) in our published  minutes  of the meeting. In this way, anyone can see the range and nature of engagement we\u2019ve conducted for policy proposals, and the rationale for our final decision. After policy development is complete, we inform our stakeholders what we\u2019ve decided. \n      \n      \n      \n       WHAT PRINCIPLES GUIDE OUR ENGAGEMENT? \n       A commitment to stakeholder engagement means addressing a number of essential questions \u2014 such as, how do we decide which groups and individuals to talk to, how do we make sure that vulnerable groups are heard, and how do we find relevant experts? \n       Our policies involve a complex balancing of values such as safety, voice, and equity. There\u2019s no simple formula for how engagement contributes to this work. But over the past year we\u2019ve developed a structure and methodology for engagement on our Community Standards, built around three core principles: \n      \n       Inclusiveness \n       Engagement broadens our perspective and creates a more inclusive approach to policy-making. \n      \n        Engagement helps us better understand how our policies impact those who use our service. When we make decisions about what content to remove and what to leave up, we affect people's speech and the way they connect on Facebook. Not everyone will agree on where we draw the lines, but at a minimum, we need to understand the concerns of those who are affected by our policies. This is particularly important for stakeholders whose voices have been marginalized. \n       Understanding how our policies affect the people who use Facebook presents a major challenge. Our scale makes it impossible to speak directly to the more than 2.7 billion people who use our platform. This dilemma also underscores the importance of reaching out to a broad spectrum of stakeholders in all regions so that our policy-making process is globally diverse. \n        The question of our impact plays out in many ways. Our policies are global (the Internet is borderless, and our mission is to build community), but we touch people's lives on a very local level. We are often asked, \u201cWhy should  you  be creating policies to govern online speech for  me ?\u201d Typically embedded in this question is a demand that we show more cultural sensitivity and understanding of regional context.  \n        Stakeholder engagement gives us a tool to deepen our local knowledge and perspective \u2013 so we can hear voices we might otherwise miss. For each policy proposal, we identify a global and diverse set of stakeholders on the issue. We seek voices across the policy spectrum, but it\u2019s not always self-evident what the spectrum is. In many cases, our policies don't line up neatly with traditional dichotomies, such as liberal versus conservative, or civil libertarian versus law enforcement. We talk to others in Facebook's Policy and Research organizations and conduct our own research to identify a range of diverse stakeholders. \n        For example, in considering how our hate speech policy should apply to certain forms of gendered language, we spoke with academic experts, women's and digital rights groups, and free speech advocates. Likewise, when considering our policy on nudity and sexual activity in art, we listened to family safety organizations, artists, and museum curators. In reviewing how our policies should apply to memorialized profiles of deceased users, we connected both with professors who study digital legacy as an academic subject and Facebook users who've been designated as \u201cLegacy Contacts\u201d and who have real world experience with this product feature. \n       It's not enough to ask how our policies affect users in general. We need to understand how our policies will impact people who are particularly vulnerable by virtue of laws, cultural practices, poverty, or other reasons that prevent them from speaking up for their rights. In our stakeholder mapping, we seek to put an emphasis on minority groups that have traditionally lacked power, such as political dissidents and religious minorities throughout the world. In reevaluating how our hate speech policy applies to certain behavioral generalizations, for example, we consulted with immigrants rights groups. Our efforts are a work in progress, but we are committed to bringing these voices into our policy discussions. \n      \n       Expertise       \n       Engagement brings expertise to our policy development process. \n        The Stakeholder Engagement team conducts detailed, iterative research to identify top subject matter experts in civil society and academia. It then gathers their views to inform our policy decisions.  \n       This engagement ensures that our policy-making process is informed by current theories and analysis, empirical research, and an understanding of the latest online trends. The expertise we gather includes issues of language, social identity, and geography, all of which bear on our policies in important ways.  \n       Facebook\u2019s policies are entwined with many complex social and technological issues, such as hate speech, terrorism, bullying and harassment, and threats of violence. Sometimes we're looking for guidance on how safety and voice should be balanced \u2014 for example, in considering what types of speech to allow about \u201cpublic figures\u201d under our policies. In other cases, we're reaching out to gain specialized knowledge, such as how our policies can draw on international human rights principles, or how minority communities may experience certain types of speech. \n        We don\u2019t have all the answers to address these problems on our platform. Sometimes the challenges we face are novel even to the experts we consult with. But by talking to outside experts and incorporating their feedback, we make our policies more thoughtful. \n       For example, over the past year and a half we've modified our  hate speech policy  to recognize three tiers of attacks. Tier 1, the most severe, involves calls to violence or dehumanizing speech against other people based on their race, ethnicity, nationality, gender or other protected characteristic (\u201cKill the Christians\u201d). Tier 2 attacks consist of statements of inferiority or expressions of contempt or disgust (\u201cMexicans are lazy\u201d). And Tier 3 covers calls to exclude or segregate (\u201cNo women allowed\u201d).  \n        Developing these tiers has made our policies more nuanced and precise. On the basis of the tiers, we're able to provide additional protections against the most harmful forms of speech. For instance, we now remove Tier 1 hate speech directed against immigrants (\u201cimmigrants are rats\u201d) but permit less intense forms of speech (\u201cimmigrants should stay out of our country\u201d) to leave room for broad political discourse.  \n        As part of our work we spoke to outside experts \u2014 academics, NGOs that study hate speech, and groups all across the political landscape. This engagement helped confirm that the tiers were comprehensive and aligned with patterns of online and offline behavior. We\u2019ll continue to consider adjustments to our policies in light of opinions from experts and civil society. \n      \n       Transparency       \n       Engagement makes our policies and our policy development process more transparent. \n       Given the impact of our Community Standards on society, it\u2019s critical for us to create a policy development process that\u2019s not only inclusive and based on expert knowledge, but also transparent. We know from talking to hundreds of stakeholders that opening up our policy-making process helps build trust. The more visibility we provide, the more our stakeholders are likely to view the Community Standards as relevant, legitimate, and based on consent.  \n       When we engage, we share details about the challenges of moderating content for 2.7 billion people and we explain the rationale behind our policies and why there may be a need for improvement. We gather stakeholder feedback so we can develop creative policy solutions to these problems. The policies we launch based on this process are still owned by Facebook, but they are stronger by virtue of having been tested through consultation and an exchange of views.  \n        It\u2019s important to acknowledge that our policies will never make everyone happy. Nudity, say, is viewed quite differently in Scandinavia and Southeast Asia, and no Facebook policy on nudity would be equally satisfactory to both. Our job as a team is to craft thoughtful global policies, knowing that our work will be criticized by some.   \n       Transparency on our process of engagement also helps us build a system of rules and enforcement that people regard as fair. We know that some people would like us to go further and disclose the names of our stakeholders and even the substance of our discussions with them. For reasons discussed above, we\u2019ve chosen not to go this route, at least for now. We'll continue to experiment with ways of being more public about engagement where we have the prior agreement of our stakeholders. \n      \n      \n     THE WAY FORWARD \n       As the breadth and specialization of our policies increase, so too will the scope of our engagement. We\u2019ll continue to refine our policy development process and work to realize our stakeholder engagement principles of inclusiveness, expertise, and transparency. We expect our team to grow, and our reach to expand.  \n       With BSR\u2019s help, we\u2019re also working on specific ways to improve.  \n       For example, we'll continue to develop means to engage with new stakeholders around the world and will seek guidance from regional experts about how to do so most effectively. We\u2019ve also had requests to explore channels like informal roundtables and recurring video-conference meetings as a way of staying in touch with stakeholders on specific policy issues. These settings provide continuity and enable us to involve stakeholders even more closely in the design of specific policies and products. \n       As we expand the scope of our outreach, we also want to investigate whether we might be able to create other mechanisms for users to give us feedback on our policies. For example, as part of the effort to gather global feedback on the Oversight Board, Facebook created a  public consultation process  containing both a questionnaire and free-form questions. Through this tool, users were able to submit their views on the Board directly to Facebook. One could imagine a similar process whereby NGOs and civil society organizations could join our network of contacts in order to receive regular policy updates and provide feedback to members of our team.   \n       These are all exciting challenges, and we look forward to working with our stakeholders to improve the level of our engagement and its contribution to the development of our policies. \n      \n     \n   \n ","policy_rationale":null}},"rawIntroContent":"\n   \n    Chaque jour, vous venez sur Facebook pour partager vos actualit\u00e9s, voir le monde tel que les autres le per\u00e7oivent, communiquer avec des amis et vous rallier \u00e0 des causes. Les conversations qui ont lieu sur Facebook refl\u00e8tent la diversit\u00e9 d\u2019une communaut\u00e9 de plus de deux\u00a0milliards de personnes qui communiquent par-del\u00e0 les pays et les cultures, dans des dizaines de langues, et qui publient toutes sortes de contenus, comme des messages, des photos et des vid\u00e9os. \n   Nous sommes conscients de l\u2019importance pour Facebook d\u2019offrir un espace encourageant les gens \u00e0 communiquer, et nous prenons tr\u00e8s au s\u00e9rieux notre r\u00f4le afin d\u2019\u00e9carter tout abus de notre service. C\u2019est la raison pour laquelle nous avons d\u00e9velopp\u00e9 un ensemble de Standards de la communaut\u00e9 qui expliquent ce qui est autoris\u00e9, ou non, sur Facebook. Nos Standards s\u2019appliquent dans le monde entier \u00e0 tous les types de contenu. Ils ont \u00e9t\u00e9 con\u00e7us pour \u00eatre exhaustifs. Par exemple, du contenu qui ne serait pas consid\u00e9r\u00e9 comme un discours haineux pourrait malgr\u00e9 tout \u00eatre supprim\u00e9 parce qu\u2019il enfreint notre r\u00e8glement sur l\u2019intimidation. \n\n     Nos Standards de la communaut\u00e9 ont pour objectif  d\u2019encourager l\u2019expression et de cr\u00e9er un environnement s\u00fbr . Nos  r\u00e8glements reposent  sur les commentaires de notre communaut\u00e9 et des experts des domaines tels que la technologie et la s\u00e9curit\u00e9 publique. Nos r\u00e8glements sont \u00e9galement ancr\u00e9s dans les principes suivants\u00a0: \n   \n      \n     S\u00e9curit\u00e9\u00a0:  chacun doit se sentir en s\u00e9curit\u00e9 pour d\u00e9velopper une communaut\u00e9. Nous nous engageons \u00e0 supprimer tout contenu qui encourage tout danger r\u00e9el, y compris (mais sans s\u2019y limiter) les blessures physiques, les pr\u00e9judices financiers et les dommages \u00e9motionnels. \n  \n        \n  \n Voix\u00a0:  notre mission consiste \u00e0 encourager la diversit\u00e9 de points de vue. Nous pr\u00e9f\u00e9rons autoriser du contenu, m\u00eame lorsque certains le trouvent r\u00e9pr\u00e9hensible, \u00e0 moins que la suppression de celui-ci puisse \u00e9viter une nuisance particuli\u00e8re. De plus, il nous arrive dans certains cas d\u2019autoriser du contenu qui \u00e0 priori serait contraire \u00e0 nos Standards si celui-ci est pertinent, significatif ou important dans l\u2019int\u00e9r\u00eat ou le d\u00e9bat public. Nous faisons ceci seulement apr\u00e8s avoir \u00e9valu\u00e9 l\u2019int\u00e9r\u00eat public du contenu et le risque de danger r\u00e9el. \n  \n    \n      \n \u00c9quit\u00e9\u00a0:  notre communaut\u00e9 est mondiale et diversifi\u00e9e. Nos r\u00e8glements peuvent para\u00eetre g\u00e9n\u00e9ralistes, mais c\u2019est parce que nous les appliquons de mani\u00e8re uniforme et \u00e9quitable \u00e0 une communaut\u00e9 qui d\u00e9passe les r\u00e9gions, les cultures et les langues. Ainsi, nos Standards de la communaut\u00e9 semblent parfois moins nuanc\u00e9s que ce que nous aimerions, ce qui donne l\u2019impression qu\u2019ils sont en contradiction avec leur objectif sous-jacent. Pour cette raison, dans certains cas, et lorsque du contexte suppl\u00e9mentaire nous est fourni, nous prenons une d\u00e9cision qui se base sur l\u2019esprit de nos politiques, plut\u00f4t qu\u2019une application \u00e0 la lettre.\n \n\n    \n     Tous les membres de Facebook ont pour responsabilit\u00e9 de pr\u00e9server s\u00e9curit\u00e9 et respect sur la plate-forme. Nous demandons \u00e0 tous de partager du contenu de mani\u00e8re responsable et de nous informer lorsque quelque chose peut enfreindre nos Standards de la communaut\u00e9. Nous aidons les gens \u00e0 signaler facilement du contenu potentiellement en infraction, y compris des Pages, des groupes, des profils, du contenu individuel ou des commentaires, afin que nous puissions l\u2019examiner. Nous permettons \u00e9galement de  bloquer , de  se d\u00e9sabonner  ou de  masquer  des personnes et des publications, afin que chacun puisse contr\u00f4ler sa propre exp\u00e9rience sur Facebook. \n\n     La violation de nos Standards de la communaut\u00e9 engendre diverses cons\u00e9quences qui varient en fonction de la gravit\u00e9 de l\u2019infraction et des ant\u00e9c\u00e9dents de l\u2019auteur sur la plate-forme. Nous pouvons, par exemple, avertir une personne d\u2019une premi\u00e8re infraction, mais si celle-ci persiste \u00e0 enfreindre notre r\u00e8glement, nous pouvons limiter sa capacit\u00e9 \u00e0 publier du contenu sur Facebook ou d\u00e9sactiver son profil. Lorsque nous pensons qu\u2019un contenu repr\u00e9sente un r\u00e9el risque de pr\u00e9judice physique ou une atteinte directe \u00e0 la s\u00e9curit\u00e9 publique, nous pouvons \u00e9galement en informer les autorit\u00e9s.  \n\n     Nos Standards de la communaut\u00e9, que nous continuerons de d\u00e9velopper au fil du temps, constituent un guide sur la fa\u00e7on de communiquer sur Facebook. C\u2019est dans cet esprit que nous demandons aux membres de la communaut\u00e9 Facebook de suivre ces r\u00e8gles. \n    \n
